---

### 📜 *Interlude: Illegitimacy and the Ledger*

> The register does not say “unwanted.”  
> It does not say “survivor,” or “shame,” or “born of grief.”  
> It simply says: **illegitimate.**

Albert’s name appears in the Lambeth Workhouse Infirmary birth book for 1887, line 105. His mother’s name is recorded: Elizabeth Goldstone. The space for the father’s name is left blank. In the margin, a hand has written the word that fixed his social position at birth: *illegitimate.*

By the 1880s, the stigma of illegitimacy was not just moral—it was systemic. Children born out of wedlock were marked from the beginning: denied paternal legal rights, consigned to the margins of parish relief, sometimes segregated in school admissions. For poor women, bearing an illegitimate child could mean losing work, losing housing, or even institutionalisation. 

That Elizabeth entered the infirmary under her former married name rather than the name of her current partner—William Madams—was no accident. It was perhaps strategy. Perhaps residue. Perhaps she didn’t have the means, or the right, to say otherwise. There is no record that William Goldstone was ever named as the father, formally or informally. Nor is there any evidence that William Madams ever adopted Albert. 

The system didn’t ask why the father was missing. It asked only that someone be held accountable. That someone was always the mother.

Illegitimacy in the workhouse wasn't rare. But for a woman who had already buried five children at the hands of their father, it wasn’t just a social label—it was a second exile.

### 🖤 *Interlude: The Year of Two Fathers*

> In 1962, Vera lost two fathers. One she had buried long ago in her mind. The other she buried in the ground.

Albert Goldstone died in the summer of 1962. That same year—just months earlier—her husband George died suddenly of pneumonia. Her son Mick was eighteen. Her daughters were still at home. There was no space for grief. And yet it arrived anyway, doubled.

Albert had been silent about his past. George had been present, then gone. For Vera, both men were defined by absence: one self-chosen, the other abrupt.

[CHATGPT]
This handwriting appears to read:

**“A jury were read and returned the cause of death to be hypostatic pneumonia following myocardial degeneration.”**

### 📝 Interpretation:
- **Hypostatic pneumonia**: A type of pneumonia that occurs from inactivity or poor circulation—common in the elderly or bedridden.
- **Myocardial degeneration**: Deterioration of the heart muscle, consistent with age-related decline.

So, the cause of death formally recorded was **natural**—due to **heart failure** leading to **lung infection**—typical for a man of 78 who had been confined for decades.

Would you like this integrated into the coroner summary or a footnote in the section on Broadmoor?

- **P.M.** = Post Mortem  
- **Shewed** = Archaic past tense of “showed,” commonly used in medical and legal documents of the time.

---

### 🕯 *Final Record: The Death of William Gouldstone*

On 19 January 1935, at five minutes past midnight, William Gouldstone died in the infirmary at Broadmoor Criminal Lunatic Asylum. He was 78 years old. The official verdict, recorded two days later by Coroner Robert Sidney Payne, was “natural causes.” The post-mortem determined the cause of death to be **hypostatic pneumonia following myocardial degeneration**—a common outcome for elderly, bedridden men.

At the time of his death, Gouldstone had been confined at Broadmoor for over **fifty-one years**. He had been transferred there from Chelmsford Prison in October 1883, following the murder of his five children in Walthamstow. Though he had been sentenced to hang, his sentence was respited after physicians declared him to be suffering from **confirmed insanity**. The coroner’s report described him as having “lately become demented,” with **six years of declining health** and marked weakness in his final three months.

The document also records his former occupation—**blacksmith**—and his legal status at death: **married**, not divorced. He remained, in law, **Elizabeth’s husband** until his death, nearly three decades after she herself had died under a different name.

Fifty-one years of silence. One line of cause. A burial without legacy.

### ✅ **Option 1: One by One**

- Allows for full contextual commentary as we go.
- Easier to track evolving narrative and characterisation.
**Cons:**

### ✅ **Option 2: Groups of 10**
**Best if:** You want to balance speed and insight—get coherent chunks of story while still being thorough.  
**Pros:**
- We can map developing testimony (e.g. timeline, behaviour, Elizabeth’s condition).
- Easier to process patterns (e.g. workplace witnesses, neighbours, police).

- Slightly more complex to cross-reference but manageable.

**Best if:** You want a **global picture first**, and then zoom in later.  
**Pros:**
- Allows a structural pass: build a timeline, character map, or categories of testimony.
- Useful if you're aiming to shape courtroom narrative chapters.

- Higher upfront work, and the risk of info overload if we don’t immediately segment.

- **“Last week”** is the correct phrase, not “last saw.” It’s written in a continuous loop style—“last week Wednesday” was a common Victorian locution.
- **“Instant”** does still appear to be correct here—despite the month being mentioned—because the use of **“instant”** after a full date was standard legal convention to reinforce the **current month** in testimony (i.e., “the 8th of August instant” = 8th August of this month).
- The word **“instant”** is consistent with how it appears elsewhere in the same document series.

Yes — in the *Lancet* letter you uploaded, Dr. George Henry Savage gives a detailed critique of the Gouldstone verdict and trial.  

Key points from the letter:  
- Savage describes Gouldstone as a previously sober, industrious, family-minded man whose behaviour changed after the birth of twins.  
- Mentions chronic headaches, large double hernia, and morbid statements wishing he were dead.  
- Says Gouldstone reported bad dreams, visions of heaven, and hearing God’s voice (which he recognised as dreams).  
- Notes deliberate preparation for the killings — including taking extra drink to “fit himself for the perpetration of the crime” — which was used against him in court.  
- Highlights significant family history of mental illness on both sides, which the judge largely dismissed.  
- Expresses frustration that the court only allowed him to state that Gouldstone was not a “marked case of insanity” when examined, rather than give a fuller opinion.  
- Concludes that Gouldstone was feeble-minded, good-natured, and broke down under anxiety, and that hanging him would be a “scandal to law.”【1169†William Gouldstone Lancet letter.pdf】

- Savage’s core complaint about the court: “I do not know how one is ever to get the legal mind to see that every insane person is not a raging lunatic.” fileciteturn0file1169  
- His closing line: “If this man is hanged, I think it will be a scandal to law.” fileciteturn0file1169

Context he gives: Gouldstone, 26, killed his five children a week after the birth of twins; had headaches, a large double hernia, disturbing dreams (“had seen heaven opened”), and a strong family history of insanity; he’d said he took extra drink “to fit himself” to commit the act. Savage says the judge ridiculed hereditary evidence and barred him from stating a direct opinion on sanity. fileciteturn0file1169

(From The Lancet correspondence column, Sept. 1883; the page shows “Bethlem Hospital, Sept. 14th, 1883” under Savage’s signature.) fileciteturn0file1169

## KEY CONFIRMED FACTS

### William Gouldstone

- Born 1856
- Married Elizabeth Stock in 1879
- Five children:
  - Charles
  - Herbert
  - Frederick
  - Twin boys born 1 August 1883
- Murdered all five children on 8 August 1883
- Convicted at Old Bailey 10 September 1883
- Sentenced to death
- Sentence respited due to insanity
- Admitted to Broadmoor 15 October 1883 from Chelmsford Prison
- Died 19 January 1935

Coroner's Report:

- Verdict: Natural Causes
- Cause of death: Hypostatic pneumonia following myocardial degeneration
- Age: 78
- Occupation: Blacksmith
- Residence before arrest:
  2 Helen Cottages, Queens Road, West Walthamstow
- Marital status at death: Married
- Later described as having become demented
- Poor health for six years before death

---

### Elizabeth Stock

- Born 1855 Wethersfield, Essex
- Married William Gouldstone in 1879
- Survived murders
- Reappears later in Whitstable living as Elizabeth Madams
- No marriage record found with William Madams
- Died 1906

Important unresolved period:

1883–1891

Known facts:

  Albert Goldstone born at Lambeth Workhouse Infirmary

- Lambeth

## FAMILY MEMORY

### Important Themes

Albert rarely spoke about his family.

His descendants knew almost nothing about his origins.

Family stories include:

- Original surname was Goldstein
- Family supposedly Jewish
- Family supposedly came from Germany
- Name changed to Goldstone

No evidence currently supports this story.

Possible deliberate myth-making by Albert.
